[845] 'Let not an eminent man (naga) dispute after having embraced those (views) separated from which he (formerly) wandered in the world; as the thorny lotus elambuga is undefiled by water and mud, so the Muni, the confessor of peace, free from greed, does not cling to sensual pleasures and the world.

12. [846] 'An accomplished man does not by (a philosophical) view, or by thinking become arrogant, for he is not of that sort; not by (holy) works, nor by tradition is he to be led, he is not led into any of the resting-places (of the mind).

13. [847] 'For him who is free from marks there are no ties, to him who is delivered by understanding there are no follies; (but those) who grasped after marks and (philosophical) views, they wander about in the world annoying (people)[146].'

 

 

10. Purābhedasutta

[pali] [than]

 

Definition of a calm Muni.

1. [848] 'With what view and with what virtue is one called calm, tell me that, O Gotama, (when) asked about the best man?'

2. [849] 'He whose desire is departed before the dissolution (of his body),' — so said Bhagavat, — 'who [163] does not depend upon beginning and end, nor reckons upon the middle, by him there is nothing preferred.[147]

3. [850] 'He who is free from anger, free from trembling, free from boasting, free from misbehaviour, he who speaks wisely, he who is not elated, he is indeed a Muni who has restrained his speech.

4. [851] 'Without desire for the future he does not grieve for the past, he sees seclusion in the phassas (touch), and he is not led by (any philosophical) views.

5. [852] 'He is unattached, not deceitful, not covetous, not envious, not impudent, not contemptuous, and not given to slander.

6. [853] 'Without desire for pleasant things and not given to conceit, and being gentle, intelligent, not credulous, he is not displeased (with anything).

7. [854] 'Not from love of gain does he learn, and he does not get angry on account of loss, and untroubled by desire he has no greed for sweet things.[148]

8. [855] 'Equable (upekhaka), always thoughtful, he does not think himself equal (to others)[149] in the world, nor distinguished, nor low: for him there are no desires (ussada).

[164] 9. [856] 'The man for whom there is nothing upon which he depends, who is independent, having understood the Dhamma, for whom there is no desire for coming into existence or leaving existence, (856)

10. [857] 'Him I call calm, not looking for sensual pleasures; for him there are no ties, he has overcome desire.

11. [858] 'For him there are no sons, cattle, fields, wealth, nothing grasped or rejected is to be found in him,

12. [859] 'That fault of which common people and Samaṇas and Brāhmaṇas say that he is possessed, is not possessed by him, therefore he is not moved by their talk.

13. [860] 'Free from covetousness, without avarice, the Muni does not reckon himself amongst the distinguished, nor amongst the plain, nor amongst the low, he does not enter time, being delivered from time.[150]

14. [861] 'He for whom there is nothing in the world (which he may call) his own, who does not grieve over what is no more, and does not walk amongst the Dhammas (after his wish), he is called calm.'[151]

 

 

11. Kalahavivadāsutta

[pali] [than]

 

The origin of contentions, disputes, etc etc

1. [862] 'Whence (do spring up) contentions and disputes, lamentation and sorrow together with envy; [165] and arrogance and conceit together with slander, whence do these spring up? pray, tell me this.'

2. [863] 'From dear (objects) spring up contentions and disputes, lamentation and sorrow together with envy; arrogance and conceit together with slander; contentions and disputes are joined with envy, and there is slander in the disputes arisen.'

3. [864] 'The dear (objects) in the world whence do they originate, and (whence) the covetousness that prevails in the world, and desire and fulfilment whence do they originate, which are (of consequence) for the future state of a man?'[152]

4. [865] 'From wish[153] originate the dear (objects) in the world, and the covetousness that prevails in the world, and desire and fulfilment originate from it, which are (of consequence) for the future state of a man.'

5. [866] 'From what has wish in the world its origin, and resolutions[154] whence do they spring, anger and falsehood and doubt, and the Dhammas which are made known by the Samaṇa (Gotama)?'

6. [867] 'What they call pleasure and displeasure in the world, by that wish springs up; having seen decay and origin in (all) bodies,[155] a person forms (his) resolutions in the world.

7. [868] 'Anger and falsehood and doubt, these Dhammas are a couple;[156] let the doubtful learn in the way of knowledge, knowingly the Dhammas have been proclaimed by the Samaṇa.'

8. [869] 'Pleasure and displeasure, whence have they [166] their origin, for want of what do these not arise? This notion which (thou mentionest), viz. "decay and origin," tell me from what does this arise.'

9. [870] 'Pleasure and displeasure have their origin from phassa (touch), when there is no touch they do not arise. This notion which (thou mentionest), viz. "decay and origin," this I tell thee has its origin from this.'

10. [871] 'From what has phassa its origin in the world and from what does grasping spring up? For want of what is there no egotism, by the cessation of what do the touches not touch? '

11. [872] 'On account of name and form the touches (exist), grasping has its origin in wish; by the cessation of wishes there is no egotism, by the cessation of form the touches do not touch.'

12. [873] 'How is one to be constituted that (his) form may cease to exist, and how do joy and pain cease to exist? Tell me this, how it ceases, that we should like to know, such was my mind?'[157]

13. [874] 'Let one not be with a natural consciousness, nor with a mad consciousness, nor without consciousness, nor with (his) consciousness gone; for him who is thus constituted form ceases to exist, for what is called delusion has its origin in consciousness.'[158](?)

14. [875] 'What we have asked thee thou hast explained [167] unto us; we will ask thee another question, answer us that: Do not some (who are considered) wise in this world tell us that the principal (thing) is the purification of the yakkha, or do they say something different from this?'[159]

15. [876] 'Thus some (who are considered) wise in this world say that the principal (thing) is the purification of the yakkha; but some of them say samaya (annihilation), the expert say (that the highest purity lies) in anupadisesa (none of the five attributes remaining).[160]

16. [877] 'And having known these to be dependent, the investigating Muni, having known the things we depend upon, and after knowing them being liberated, does not enter into dispute, the wise (man) does not go to reiterated existence.'[161]

 

 

12. Kūlaviyūhasutta

[pali] [than]

 

A description of disputing philosophers. The different schools of philosophy contradict each other, they proclaim different truths, but the truth is only one. As long as the disputations are going on, so long will there be strife in the world.

1. [878] Abiding by their own views, some (people), having got into contest, assert themselves to be [168] the (only) expert (saying), '(He) who understands this, he knows the Dhamma; he who reviles this, he is not perfect.'[162]

2. [879] So having got into contest they dispute: 'The opponent (is) a fool, an ignorant (person),' so they say. Which one of these, pray, is the true doctrine (vada)? for all these assert themselves (to be the only) expert.

3. [880] He who does not acknowledge an opponent's doctrine (dhamma), he is a fool, a beast, one of poor understanding, all are fools with a very poor understanding; all these abide by their (own) views.

4. [881] They are surely purified by their own view, they are of a pure understanding, expert, thoughtful, amongst them there is no one of poor understanding, their view is quite perfect!

5. [882] I do not say, 'This is the reality,' which fools say mutually to each other; they made their own views the truth, therefore they hold others to be fools.

6. [883] What some say is the truth, the reality, that others say is void, false, so having disagreed they dispute. Why do not the Samaṇas say one (and the same thing)?

7. [884] For the truth is one, there is not a second, about which one intelligent man might dispute with another intelligent man; (but) they themselves praise different truths, therefore the Samaṇas do not say one and the same thing).[163]

[169] 8. [885] Why do the disputants that assert themselves (to be the only) expert, proclaim different truths? Have many different truths been heard of, or do they (only) follow (their own) reasoning?

9. [886] There are not many different truths in the world, no eternal ones except consciousness; but having reasoned on the (philosophical) views they proclaim a double Dhamma, truth and falsehood.[164]

10. [887] In regard to what has been seen, or heard, virtue and (holy) works, or what has been thought, and on account of these (views) looking (upon others) with contempt, standing in (their) resolutions joyful, they say that the opponent is a fool and an ignorant person (?)[165]

11. [888] Because he holds another (to be) a fool, therefore he calls himself expert, in his own opinion he is one that tells what is propitious, others he blames, so he said.[166] (?)

12. [889] He is full of his overbearing (philosophical) view, mad with pride, thinking himself perfect, he is in his own opinion anointed with the spirit (of genius), for his (philosopbical) view is quite complete.

[170] 13. [890] If he according to another's report is low, then (he says) the other is also of a low understanding, and if he himself is accomplished and wise, there is not any fool amongst the Samaṇas.[167]

14. [891] 'Those who preach a doctrine (dhamma) different from this, fall short of purity and are imperfect,' so the Titthiyas say repeatedly, for they are inflamed by passion for their own (philosophical) views.

15. [892] Here they maintain purity, in other doctrines (dhamma) they do not allow purity; so the Titthiyas, entering extensively (upon details), say that in their own way there is something firm.

16. [893] And saying that there is something firm in his own way he holds his opponent to be a fool; thus he himself brings on strife, calling his opponent a fool and impure (asuddhadhamma).

17. [894] Standing in (his) resolution, having himself measured (teachers, etc), he still more enters into dispute in the world; but having left all resolutions nobody will excite strife in the world.[168]

[171]

 

 

13. Mahāviyūhasutta

[pali] [than]

 

Philosophers cannot lead to purity, they only praise themselves and stigmatise others. But a Brāhmaṇa has overcome all dispute, he is indifferent to learning, he is appeased.

1. [895] Those who abiding in the (philosophical) views dispute, saying, 'This is the truth,' they all incur blame, and they also obtain praise in this matter.

2. [896] This is little, not enough to (bring about) tranquillity, I say there are two fruits of dispute; having seen this let no one dispute, understanding Khema (i.e. Nibbāna) to be the place where there is no dispute.

3. [897] The opinions that have arisen amongst people, all these the wise man does not embrace; he is independent. Should he who is not pleased with what has been seen and heard resort to dependency?[169] (?)

4. [898] Those who consider virtue the highest of all, say that purity is associated with restraint; having taken upon themselves a (holy) work they serve. Let us learn in this (view), then, his (the Master's) purity; wishing for existence they assert themselves to be the only expert.[170]

5. [899] If he falls off from virtue and (holy) works, he trembles, having missed (his) work; he laments, he [172] prays for purity in this world, as one who has lost his caravan or wandered away from his house.

6. [900] Having left virtue and (holy) works altogether, and both wrong and blameless work, not praying for purity or impurity, he wanders abstaining (from both purity and impurity), without having embraced peace.

7. [901] By means of penance, or anything disliked, or what has been seen, or heard, or thought, going upwards they wail for what is pure, without being free from desire for reiterated existence.

8. [902] For him who wishes (for something there always are) desires,[171] and trembling in (the midst of his) plans; he for whom there is no death and no re-birth, how can he tremble or desire anything?

9. [903] What some call the highest Dhamma, that others again call wretched; which one of these, pray, is the true doctrine (vada)? for all these assert themselves (to be the only) expert.

10. [904] Their own Dhamma they say is perfect, another's Dhamma again they say is wretched; so having disagreed they dispute, they each say their own opinions (are) the truth.

11. [905] If one (becomes) low by another's censure, then there will be no one distinguished amongst the Dhammas; for they all say another's Dhamma (is) low, in their own they say there is something firm.[172]

[173] 12. [906] The worshipping of their own Dhamma is as great as their praise of their own ways; all schools would be in the same case, for their purity is individual.[173]

13. [907] There is nothing about a Brāhmaṇa dependent upon others, nothing amongst the Dhammas which he would embrace after investigation; therefore he has overcome the disputes, for he does not regard any other Dhamma as the best.

14. [908] 'I understand, I see likewise this,' so saying, some by (their philosophical) views return to purity. If he saw purity, what then (has been effected) by another's view? Having conquered they say that purity exists by another.[174] (?)

15. [909] A seeing man will see name and form, and having seen he will understand those (things); let him at pleasure see much or little, for the expert do not say that purity exists by that.

16. [910] A dogmatist is no leader to purity, being guided by prejudiced views, saying that good consists in what he is given to, and saying that purity is there, he saw the thing so.[175]

17. [911] A Brāhmaṇa does not enter time, (or) the [174] number (of living beings), (he is) no follower of (philosophical) views, nor a friend of knowledge; and having penetrated the opinions that have arisen amongst people, he is indifferent to learning, while others acquire it.

18. [912] The Muni, having done away with ties here in the world, is no partisan in the disputes that have arisen; appeased amongst the unappeased he is indifferent, not embracing learning, while others acquire it.

19. [913] Having abandoned his former passions, not contracting new ones, not wandering according to his wishes, being no dogmatist, he is delivered from the (philosophical) views, being wise, and he does not cling to the world, neither does he blame himself.

20. [914] Being secluded amongst all the doctrines (dhamma), whatever has been seen, heard, or thought, he is a Muni who has laid down his burden and is liberated, not belonging to time (na kappiyo), not dead, not wishing for anything. So said Bhagavat.

 

 

14. Tuvatakasutta

[pali] [than]

 

How a Bhikkhu attains bliss, what his duties are, and what he is to avoid.

1. [915] 'I ask thee, who art a kinsman of the Ādikkas and a great Isi, about seclusion (viveka) and the state of peace. How is a Bhikkhu, after having seen it, extinguished, not grasping at anything in the world?'

[175] 2. [916] 'Let him completely cut off the root of what is called papanka[176] (delusion), thinking "I am wisdom;"' — so said Bhagavat, — 'all the desires that arise inwardly, let him learn to subdue them, always being thoughtful.

3. [917] 'Let him learn every Dhamma inwardly or outwardly; let him not therefore be proud, for that is not called bliss by the good.

4. [918] 'Let him not therefore think himself better (than others or) low or equal (to others); questioned by different people, let him not adorn himself.[177]

5. [919] 'Let the Bhikkhu be appeased inwardly, let him not seek peace from any other (quarter); for him who is inwardly appeased there is nothing grasped or rejected.

6. [920] 'As in the middle (i.e. depth) of the sea no wave is born, (but as it) remains still,[178] so let the Bhikkhu be still,[178] without desire, let him not desire anything whatever.'

7. [921] He with open eyes expounded clearly the Dhamma that removes (all) dangers; tell (now) the religious practices; the precepts or contemplation.[179]

8. [922] Bhagavat: 'Let him not be greedy with his eyes, let him keep his ears from the talk of the town, let him not be greedy after sweet things, and let him not desire anything in the world.

9. [923] 'When he is touched by the touch (of illness), [176] let the Bhikkhu not lament, and let him not wish for existence anywhere, and let him not tremble at dangers.

10. [924] 'Having obtained boiled rice and drink, solid food and clothes, let him not store up (these things), and let him not be anxious, if he does not get them.

11. [925] 'Let him be meditative, not prying, let him abstain from misbehaviour,[180] let him not be indolent, let the Bhikkhu live in his quiet dwelling.

12. [926] 'Let him not sleep too much, let him apply himself ardently to watching, let him abandon sloth, deceit, laughter, sport, sexual intercourse, and adornment.

13. [927] 'Let him not apply himself to practising (the hymns of) the Athabbana(-veda), to (the interpretation of) sleep and signs, nor to astrology; let not (my) follower (mamaka) devote himself to (interpreting) the cry of birds, to causing impregnation, nor to (the art of) medicine.

14. [928] 'Let the Bhikkhu not tremble at blame, nor puff himself up when praised; let him drive off covetousness together with avarice, anger, and slander.

15. [929] 'Let the Bhikkhu not be engaged in purchase and sale, let him not blame others in anything, let him not scold in the village, let him not from love of gain speak to people.

16. [930] 'Let not the Bhikkhu be a boaster, and let him not speak coherent[181] language; let him not learn pride, let him not speak quarrelsome language.

[177] 17. [931] 'Let him not be led into falsehood, let him not consciously do wicked things; and with respect to livelihood, understanding, virtue, and (holy) works let him not despise others.

18. [932] 'Having heard much talk from much-talking Samaṇas let him not irritated answer them with harsh language; for the good do not thwart[182] others.

19. [933] 'Having understood this Dhamma, let the investigating and always thoughtful Bhikkhu learn; having conceived bliss to consist in peace, let him not be indolent in Gotama's commandments.

20. [934] 'For he a conqueror unconquered saw the Dhamma visibly, without any traditional instruction;[183] therefore let him learn, heedful in his, Bhagavat's, commandments, and always worshipping.'

 

 

15. Attadaṇḍasutta

[pali] [than]

 

Description of an accomplished Muni.

1. [935] From him who has seized a stick fear arises. Look at people killing (each other); I will tell of grief as it is known to me.

2. [936] Seeing people struggling like fish in (a pond with) little water, seeing them obstructed by each other, a fear came over me.

3. [937] The world is completely unsubstantial, all quarters are shaken; wishing for a house for myself I did not see (one) uninhabited.

4. [938] But having seen (all beings) in the end obstructed, discontent arose in me; then I saw in [178] this world an arrow, difficult to see, stuck in the heart.

5. [939] He who has been pierced by this arrow runs through all quarters; but having drawn out that arrow, he will not run, he will sit down (quietly).

6. [940] There (many) studies are gone through; what is tied in the world let him not apply himself to (untie) it; having wholly transfixed desire, let him learn his own extinction (Nibbāna).

7. [941] Let the Muni be truthful, without arrogance, undeceitful, free from slander, not angry, let him overcome avarice.

8. [942] Let the man who has turned his mind to Nibbāna conquer sleepiness, drowsiness, and sloth; let him not live together with indolence, let him not indulge in conceit.

9. [943] Let him not be led into falsehood, let him not turn his affection to form; let him penetrate arrogance, let him wander abstaining from violence.

10. [944] Let him not delight in what is old, let him not bear with what is new, let him not grieve for what is lost, let him not give himself up to desire.[184]

11. [945] (This desire) I call greed, the great stream, I call (it) precipitation, craving, a trouble, a bog of lust difficult to cross.[185]

12. [946] The Muni who without deviating from truth [179] stands fast on the firm ground (of Nibbāna, being) a Brāhmaṇa, he, having forsaken everything, is indeed called calm.

13. [947] He indeed is wise, he is accomplished, having understood the Dhamma independent (of everything); wandering rightly in the world he does not envy any one here.

14. [948] Whosoever has here overcome lust, a tie difficult to do away with in the world, he does not grieve, he does not covet,[186] having cut off the stream, and being without bonds.

15. [949] What is before (thee), lay that aside; let there be nothing behind thee; if thou wilt not grasp after what is in the middle, thou wilt wander calm.[187]

16. [950] The man who has no desire at all for name and form (individuality) and who does not grieve over what is no more, he indeed does not decay in the world.[188]

17. [951] He who does not think, 'this is mine' and 'for others there is also something,' he, not having egotism, does not grieve at having nothing.[189]

18. [952] Not being harsh, not greedy, being without desire, and being the same under all circumstances (samo[190]), — that I call a good result, when asked about an undaunted man.

19. [953] For him who is free from desire, for the [180] discerning (man) there is no Saŋkhāra; abstaining from every sort of effort he sees happiness everywhere.[191]

20. [954] The Muni does not reckon himself amongst the plain, nor amongst the low, nor amongst the distinguished; being calm and free from avarice, he does not grasp after nor reject anything.[192]

 

 

16. Sāriputtasutta

[pali] [than]

 

On Sāriputta asking what a Bhikkhu is to devote himself to, Buddha shows what life he is to lead.

1. [955] 'Neither has before been seen by me,' — so said the venerable Sāriputta, — 'nor has any one heard of such a beautifully-speaking master, a teacher arrived from the Tusita heaven.

2. [956] 'As he, the clearly-seeing, appears to the world of men and gods, after having dispelled all darkness, so he wanders alone in the midst (of people).

3. [957] 'To this Buddha, who is independent, unchanged, a guileless teacher, who has arrived (in the world), I have come supplicatingly with a question[193] from many who are bound in this world.

4. [958] 'To a Bhikkhu who is loath (of the world) and affects an isolated seat, the root of a tree or a cemetery, or (who lives) in the caves of the mountains,

[181] 5. [959] 'How many dangers (are there not) in these various dwelling-places at which the Bhikkhu does not tremble in his quiet dwelling! (959)

6. [960] 'How many dangers (are there not) in the world for him who goes to the immortal region,[194] (dangers) which the Bhikkhu overcomes in his distant dwelling!

7. [961] 'Which are his words, which are his objects in this world, which are the virtue and (holy) works of the energetic Bhikkhu?

8. [962] 'What study having devoted himself to, intent on one object,[195] wise and thoughtful, can he blow off his own filth as the smith (blows off) that of the silver?'[196]

9. [963] 'What is pleasant for him who is disgusted (with birth, etc), O Sāriputta,' — so said Bhagavat, — 'if he cultivates a lonely dwelling-place, and loves perfect enlightenment in accordance with the Dhamma, that I will tell thee as I understand it.

10. [964] 'Let not the wise and thoughtful Bhikkhu wandering on the borders[197] be afraid of the five dangers: gad-flies and (all other) flies,[198] snakes, contact with (evil) men,[199] and quadrupeds.

11. [965] 'Let him not be afraid of adversaries,[200] even having seen many dangers from them; further he [182] will overcome other dangers while seeking what is good.

12. [966] 'Touched by sickness and hunger let him endure cold and excessive heat, let him, touched by them in many ways, and being houseless, make strong exertions.

13. [967] 'Let him not commit theft, let him not speak falsely, let him touch friendly what is feeble or strong, what he acknowledges to be the agitation of the mind, let him drive that off as a partisan of Kanha (i.e. Māra).

14. [968] 'Let him not fall into the power of anger and arrogance; having dug up the root of these, let him live, and let him overcome both what is pleasant and what is unpleasant.

15. [969] 'Guided by wisdom, taking delight in what is good, let him scatter those dangers, let him overcome discontent in his distant dwelling, let him overcome the four causes of lamentation.

16. [970] 'What shall I eat, or where shall I eat? — he lay indeed uncomfortably (last night) — where shall I lie this night? let the Sekha who wanders about houseless subdue these lamentable doubts.

17. [971] 'Having had in (due) time both food and clothes, let him know moderation in this world for the sake of happiness; guarded in these (things) and wandering restrained in the village let him, even (if he be) irritated, not speak harsh words.

18. [972] 'Let him be with down-cast eyes, and not prying, devoted to meditation, very watchful; having acquired equanimity let him with a composed mind cut off the seat of doubt, and misbehaviour.

19. [973] 'Urged on by words (of his teachers) let him be thoughtful and rejoice (at this urging), let [183] him break stubbornness in his fellow-students, let him utter propitious words and not unseasonable, let him not think detractingly of others.

20. [974] 'And then the five impurities in the world, the subjection of which he must learn thoughtfully, — let him overcome passion for form, sound and taste, smell and touch.

21. [975] 'Let the Bhikkhu subdue his wish for these Dhammas and be thoughtful, and with his mind well liberated, then in time he will, reflecting upon Dhamma, and having become intent upon one object, destroy darkness.' So said Bhagavat.

[184]

 

 

V. PĀRĀYANA VAGGA

 

 

1. Vatthugāthā

[pali]

 

To the Brāhmaṇa Bāvarī, living on the banks of the Godhāvaī, in Assaka's territory, comes another Brāhmaṇa and asks for five hundred pieces of money, but not getting them he curses Bāvarī, saying, 'May thy head on the seventh day hence cleave into seven.' A deity comforts Bāvarī by referring him to Buddha. Then Bāvarī sends his sixteen disciples to Buddha, and each of thern asks Buddha a question.

1. [976] From the beautiful city of the Kosalas (Sāvatthī) a Brāhmaṇa, well versed in the hymns, went to the South (Dakkhiṇāpatha) wishing for nothingness.[201]

2. [977] In Assaka's territory, in the neighbourhood of Alaka, he dwelt on the banks of the Godhāvaī, (living) on gleanings and fruit.

3. [978] And close by the bank there was a large village, with the income of which he prepared a great sacrifice.

4. [979] Having offered the great sacrifice, he again entered the hermitage. Upon his re-entering, another Brāhmaṇa arrived,

5. [980] With swollen feet,[202] trembling, covered with mud, with dust on his head. And he going up [185] to him (i.e. the first Brāhmaṇa) demanded five hundred (pieces of money).

6. [981] Bāvarī, seeing him, bade him be seated, asked him whether he was happy and well, and spoke as follows:

7. [982] 'What gifts I had are all given away by me; pardon me, O Brāhmaṇa, I have no five hundred.'

8. [983] 'If thou wilt not give to me who asks, may thy head on the seventh clay cleave into seven.'

9. [984] So after the usual ceremonies this impostor made known his fearful (curse). On hearing these his words Bāvarī became sorrowful.

10. [985] He wasted away taking no food, transfixed by the arrow of grief, but yet his mind delighted in meditation.

11. [986] Seeing Bāvarī struck with horror and sorrowful, the benevolent deity (of that place) approached him and said as follows:

12. [987] 'He does not know (anything about) the head; he is a hypocrite coveting riches; knowledge of the head and head-splitting is not found in him.'[203]

13. [988] 'If the venerable (deity) knows it, then tell me, when asked, all about the head and head-splitting; let us hear thy words.'

14. [989] 'I do not know this; knowledge of it is not found in me; as to the head and head-splitting, this is to be seen by Buddhas (only).'

15. [990] 'Who then, say, in the circumference of the [186] earth knows the head and head-splitting, tell me that, O deity?'

16. [991] 'Formerly went out from Kapilavatthu a ruler of the world, an offspring of the Okkaka king, the Sakya son, the light-giving;

17. [992] 'He is, O Brāhmaṇa, the perfectly Enlightened (Sambuddha); perfect in all things, he has attained the power of all knowledge, sees clearly in everything; he has arrived at the destruction of all things, and is liberated in the destruction of the upadhis.[204]

18. [993] 'He is Buddha, he is Bhagavat in the world, he, the clearly-seeing, teaches the Dhamma; go thou to him and ask, he will explain it to thee.'

19. [994] Having heard the word 'Sambuddha,' Bāvarī rejoiced, his grief became little, and he was filled with great delight.

20. [995] Bāvarī glad, rejoicing, and eager asked the deity: 'In what village or in what town or in what province dwells the chief of the world, that going there we may adore the perfectly Enlightened, the first of men?'

21. [996] 'In Sāvatthī, the town of the Kosalas, dwells Gina (the Victorious), of great understanding and excellent wide knowledge, he the Sakya son, unyoked, free from passion, skilled in head-splitting, the bull of men.'

22. [997] Then (Bāvarī) addressed his disciples, Brāhmaṇas, perfect in the hymns: 'Come, youths, I will tell (you something), listen to my words:

23. [998] 'He whose appearance in the world is difficult to be met with often, he is at the present time[205] [187] born in the world and widely renowned as Sambuddha (the perfectly Enlightened); go quickly to Sāvatthī and behold the best of men.'

24. [999] 'How then can we know, on seeing him, that he is Buddha, O Brāhmaṇa? Tell us who do not know him, by what may we recognise him?

25. [1000] 'For in the hymns are to be found the marks of a great man, and thirty-two are disclosed altogether, one by one.'

26. [1001] 'For him on whose limbs these marks of a great man are to be found, there are two ways left, a third does not exist.

27. [1002] 'If he abides in a dwelling, he will subdue this earth without rod (or) sword, he will rule with justice.

28. [1003] 'And if he departs from his dwelling for the wilderness, he becomes the saint, incomparable Sambuddha, who has removed the veil (from the world).[206]

29. [1004] 'Ask in your mind about my birth and family, my marks, hymns, and my other disciples, the head and head-splitting.

30. [1005] 'If he is Buddha, the clear-sighted, then he will answer by word of mouth the questions you have asked in your mind.'

31., [1006] 32., [1007] 33. [1008] Having heard Bāvarī's words his disciples, sixteen Brāhmaṇas, Agita, Tissametteyya, Puṇṇaka, further Mettagū, Dhotaka and Upasīva, and Nanda, further Hemaka, the two Todeyya and Kappa, and the wise Gatukaṇṇī, Bhadrāvudha and Udaya, and also the Brāhmaṇa Posāla, and the wise Mogharāgan, and the great Isi Piŋgiya.

34. [1009] All of them, having each their host (of pupils), [188] and being themselves widely renowned throughout the world, thinkers delighting in meditation, wise, scented with the perfume of former (good deeds).[207]

35. [1010] Having saluted Bāvarī and gone round him towards the right, all with matted hair and bearing hides, departed with their faces turned to the north.

36. [1011] To Patiṭṭhāna of Aḷaka first, then to Māhissatī, and also to Uggenī, Gonaddha, Vedisā, Vanasavhaya,

37. [1012] And also to Kosambī, Sāketa, and Sāvatthī, the most excellent of cities, to Setavya, Kapilavatthu, and the city of Kusināra,

38. [1013] And to Pāva, the city of wealth, to Vesālī, the city of Magadha, to Pāasāṇaka Ketiya (the Rock Temple), the lovely, the charming.

39. [1014] As he who is athirst (longs for) the cold water, as the merchant (longs for) gain, as he who is plagued by heat (longs for) shade, so in haste they ascended the mountain.

40. [1015] And Bhagavat at that time attended by the assembly of the Bhikkhus taught the Dhamma to the Bhikkhus, and roared like a lion in the forest.

41. [1016] Agita beheld Sambuddha as the shining (sun) without (burning) rays, as the moon on the fifteenth, having reached her plenitude.

42. [1017] Then observing his limbs and all the marks in their fullness, standing apart, rejoiced, he asked the questions of his mind: —

43. [1018] 'Tell me about (my master's) birth, tell me about his family together with the marks, tell me about his perfection in the hymns, how many (hymns) does the Brāhmaṇa recite?'

[189] 44. [1019] Bhagavat said: 'One hundred and twenty years (is his) age, and by family he is a Bāvarī; three are his marks on the limbs, and in the three Vedas he is perfect. (1019)

45. [1020] 'In the marks and in the Itihāsa together with Nighaṇḍu and Keṭubha — he recites five hundred — and in his own Dhamma he has reached perfection.'

46. [1021] Agita thought: 'Explain fully the marks of Bāvarī, O thou best of men, who cuts off desire; let there be no doubt left for us.'

47. [1022] Bhagavat said: 'He covers his face with his tongue, he has a circle of hair between the eye-brows, (his) privy member (is) hidden in a sheath, know this, O young man.'[208]

48. [1023] Not hearing him ask anything, but hearing the questions answered, the multitude reflected overjoyed and with joined hands: —

49. [1024] 'Who, be he a god, or Brahman, or Inda, the husband of Suga, asked in his mind those questions, and to whom did that (speech) reply?'

50. [1025] Agita said: 'The head and head-splitting Bāvarī asked about; explain that, O Bhagavat, remove our doubt, O Isi.'

51. [1026] Bhagavat said: 'Ignorance is the head, know this; knowledge cleaves the head, together with belief, thoughtfulness, meditation, determination, and strength.'

52. [1027] Then with great joy having composed himself the young man put his hide on one shoulder, [190] fell at (Bhagavat's) feet (and saluted him) with his head, (saying):

53. [1028] 'Bāvarī, the Brāhmaṇa, together with his disciples, O thou venerable man, delighted and glad, does homage to thy feet, O thou clearly-seeing.'

54. [1029] Bhagavat said: 'Let Bāvarī, the Brāhmaṇa, be glad together with his disciples! Be thou also glad, live long, O young man!

55. [1030] 'For Bāvarī and for thee, for all there are all (kinds of) doubt; having got an opportunity, ask ye whatever you wish.'

56. [1031] After getting permission from Sambuddha, Agita sitting there with folded hands asked Tathāgata the first question.

 

 

2. Agitamāṇavapukkhā

[pali] [than]

 

1. [1032] 'By what is the world shrouded,' — so said the venerable Agita, — 'by what does it not shine? What callest thou its pollution, what is its great danger?'

2. [1033] 'With ignorance is the world shrouded, O Agita,' — so said Bhagavat, — 'by reason of avarice it does not shine; desire I call its pollution, pain is its great danger.'

3. [1034] 'The streams of desire flow in every direction,' — so said the venerable Agita; — 'what dams the streams, say what restrains the streams, by what may the streams be shut off?'[209]

[191] 4. [1035] 'Whatever streams there are in the world, O Agita,' — so said Bhagavat, — 'thoughtfulness is their dam, thoughtfulness I call the restraint of the streams, by understanding they are shut off.'

5. [1036] 'Both understanding and thoughtfulness,' — so said the venerable Agita, — 'and name and shape,[210] O venerable man, — asked about this by me, declare by what is this stopped?'

6. [1037] Buddha: 'This question which thou hast asked, O Agita, that I will explain to thee; (I will explain to thee) by what name and shape[211] are totally stopped; by the cessation of consciousness this is stopped here.'

7. [1038] Agita: 'Those who have examined (all) Dhammas (i.e. the saints), and those who are disciples, (and those who are) common men here, — when thou art asked about their mode of life, declare it unto me, thou who art wise, O venerable man.'

8. [1039] Buddha: 'Let the Bhikkhu not crave for sensual pleasures, let him be calm in mind, let him wander about skilful in all Dhammas, and thoughtful.'

 

 

3. Tissametteyyamāṇavapukkhā

[pali] [than]

 

1. [1040] 'Who is contented in the world,' — so said the venerable Tissametteyya, — 'who is without commotions? Who after knowing both ends does not stick in the middle, as far as his understanding is [192] concerned? Whom dost thou call a great man? Who has overcome desire in this world?'

2. [1041] 'The Bhikkhu who abstains from sensual pleasures, O Metteyya,' — so said Bhagavat, — 'who is free from desire, always thoughtful, happy by reflection, he is without commotions, he after knowing both ends does not stick in the middle, as far as his understanding is concerned; him I call a great man; he has overcame desire in this world.'

 

4. Puṇṇakamāṇavapukkhā

[pali] [than]

 

1. [1042] 'To him who is without desire, who has seen the root (of sin),' — so said the venerable Punnaka, — 'I have come supplicatingly with a question: on account of what did the Isis and men, Khattiyas and Brāhmaṇas, offer sacrifices to the gods abundantly in this world? (about this) I ask thee, O Bhagavat, tell me this.'

2. [1043] 'All these Isis and men, Khattiyas and Brāhmaṇas, O Punnaka,' — so said Bhagavat, — 'who offered sacrifices to the gods abundantly in this world, offered sacrifices, O Punnaka, after reaching old age, wishing for their present condition.'

3. [1044] 'All these Isis and men, Khattiyas and Brāhmaṇas,' — so said the venerable Punnaka, — 'who offered sacrifices to the gods abundantly in this world, did they, O Bhagavat, indefatigable in the way of offering, cross over both birth and old age, O venerable man? I ask thee, O Bhagavat, tell me this.'

[193] 4. [1045] 'They wished for, praised, desired, abandoned (sensual pleasures), O Punnaka,' — so said Bhagavat, — 'they desired sensual pleasures on account of what they reached by them; they, devoted to offering, dyed with the passions of existence, did not cross over birth and old age, so I say.'

5. [1046] 'If they, devoted to offering,' — so said the venerable Punnaka, — 'did not by offering cross over birth and old age, O venerable man, who then in the world of gods and men crossed over birth and old age, O venerable man, I ask thee, O Bhagavat, tell me this?'

6. [1047] 'Having considered everything[212] in the world, O Punnaka,' — so said Bhagavat, — 'he who is not defeated anywhere in the world, who is calm without the smoke of passions, free from woe, free from desire, he crossed over birth and old age, so I say.'

 

5. Mettagūmāṇavapukkhā

[pali] [than]

 

1. [1048] 'I ask thee, O Bhagavat, tell me this,' — so said the venerable Mettagū, — 'I consider thee accomplished and of a cultivated mind, why are these (creatures), whatsoever they are of many kinds in the world, always subject to pain?

2. [1049] Thou mayest well ask me concerning the origin of pain, O Mettagū,' — so said Bhagavat, —
[194] 'I will explain that to thee in the way I myself know it: originating in the upadhis pains arise, whatsoever they are, of many kinds in the world.

3. [1050] 'He who being ignorant creates upadhi, that fool again undergoes pain; therefore let not the wise man create upadhi, considering (that this is) the birth and origin of pain.'

4. [1051] Mettagū: 'What we have asked thee thou hast explained to us; another (question) I ask thee, answer that, pray: How do the wise cross the stream, birth and old age, and sorrow and lamentation? Explain that thoroughly to me, O Muni, for this thing (dhamma) is well known to thee.'

5. [1052] 'I will explain the Dhamma to thee, O Mettagū,' — so said Bhagavat, — 'if a man in the visible world, without any traditional instruction, has understood it, and wanders about thoughtful, he may overcome desire in the world.'

6. [1053] Mettagū: 'And I take a delight in that, in the most excellent Dhamma, O great Isi, which if a man has understood, and he wanders about thoughtful, he may overcome desire in the world.'[213]

7. [1054] 'Whatsoever thou knowest, O Mettagū,' — so said Bhagavat, — '(of what is) above, below, across, and in the middle, taking no delight and no rest in these things, let thy mind not dwell on existence.

8. [1055] 'Living so, thoughtful, strenuous, let the Bhikkhu wandering about, after abandoning selfishness, birth, [195] and old age, and sorrow, and lamentation, being a wise man, leave pain in this world.'

9. [1056] Mettagū: 'I delight in these words of the great Isi; well expounded, O Gotama, is (by thee) freedom from upadhi (i.e. Nibbāna). Bhagavat in truth has left pain, for this Dhamma is well known to thee.[214]

10. [1057] 'And those also will certainly leave pain whom thou, O Muni, constantly mayest admonish; therefore I bow down to thee, having come hither, O chief (naga), may Bhagavat also admonish me constantly.'

11. [1058] Buddha: 'The Brāhmaṇa whom I may acknowledge as accomplished, possessing nothing, not cleaving to the world of lust, he surely has crossed this stream, and he has crossed over to the other shore, free from harshness (akhila), (and) free from doubt.

12. [1059] 'And he is a wise and accomplished man in this world; having abandoned this cleaving to reiterated existence he is without desire, free from woe, free from longing, he has crossed over birth and old age, so I say.'

[196]

 

6. Dhotakamāṇavapukkhā

[pali] [than]

 

1. [1060] 'I ask thee, O Bhagavat, tell me this,' — so said the venerable Dhotaka, — 'I long for thy word, O great Isi; let one, having listened to thy utterance, learn his own extinction.'

2. [1061] 'Exert thyself then, O Dhotaka,' — so said Bhagavat, — 'being wise and thoughtful in this world, let one, having listened to my utterance, learn his own extinction.'

3. [1062] Dhotaka: 'I see in the world of gods and men a Brāhmaṇa wandering about, possessing nothing; therefore I bow down to thee, O thou all-seeing one, free me, O Sakka, from doubts.'

4. [1063] Buddha: 'I shall not go to free any one in the world who is doubtful, O Dhotaka; when thou hast learned the best Dhamma, then thou shalt cross this stream.'[215]

5. [1064] Dhotaka: 'Teach (me), O Brāhmaṇa, having compassion (on me), the Dhamma of seclusion (i.e. Nibbāna), that I may understand (it and) that I, without falling into many shapes like the air, may wander calm and independent in this world.'[216] (?)

[197] 6. [1065] 'I will explain to thee peace[217], O Dhotaka,' — so said Bhagavat; — 'if a man in the visible world, without any traditional instruction, has understood it, and wanders about thoughtful, he may overcome desire in the world.'

7. [1066] Dhotaka: 'And I take delight in that, the highest peace,[218] O great Isi, which if a man has understood, and he wanders about thoughtful, he may overcome desire in the world.'

8. [1067] 'Whatsoever thou knowest, O Dhotaka,' — so said Bhagavat, — '(of what is) above, below, across, and in the middle, knowing this to be a tie in the world, thou must not thirst for reiterated existence.'

 

7. Upasīvamāṇavapukkhā

[pali] [than]

 

1. [1068] 'Alone, O Sakka; and without assistance I shall not be able to cross the great stream,' — so said the venerable Upasīva; — 'tell me an object, O thou all-seeing one, by means of which one may cross this stream.'

2. [1069] 'Having in view nothingness, being thoughtful, O Upasīva,' — so said Bhagavat, — 'by the reflection of nothing existing shalt thou cross the stream; having abandoned sensual pleasures, being loath of doubts, thou shalt regard the extinction of desire (i.e. Nibbāna), both day and night.'[219]

[198] 3. [1070] Upasīva: 'He whose passion for all sensual pleasures has departed, having resorted to nothingness, after leaving everything else, and being delivered in the highest deliverance by knowledge, will he remain there without proceeding further?'[220]

4. [1071] 'He whose passion for all sensual pleasures has departed, O Upasīva,' — so said Bhagavat, — 'having resorted to nothingness after leaving everything else, and being delivered in the highest deliverance by knowledge, he will remain there without proceeding further.'

5. [1072] Upasīva: 'If he remains there without proceeding further for a multitude of years, O thou all-seeing one, (and if) he becomes there tranquil and delivered, will there be consciousness for such a one?'[221]

6. [1073] 'As a flame blown about by the violence of the wind, O Upasīva,' — so said Bhagavat, — 'goes out, cannot be reckoned (as existing), even so a Muni, delivered from name and body, disappears, and cannot be reckoned (as existing).'[222]

7. [1074] Upasīva: 'Has he (only) disappeared, or does he not exist (any longer), or is he for ever free [199] from sickness? Explain that thoroughly to me, O Muni, for this Dhamma is well known to thee.'[223]

8. [1075] 'For him who has disappeared there is no form, O Upasīva,' — so said Bhagavat, — 'that by which they say he is, exists for him no longer, when all things (dhamma) have been cut off, all (kinds of) dispute are also cut off.'[224]

 

8. Nandamāṇavapukkhā

[pali] [than]

 

1. [1076] 'There are Munis in the world,' — so said the venerable Nanda, — 'so people say. How is this (understood) by thee? Do they call him a Muni who is possessed of knowledge or him who is possessed of life?'[225]

2. [1077] Buddha: 'Not because of (any philosophical) view, nor of tradition, nor of knowledge, O Nanda, do the expert call (any one) a Muni; (but) such as wander free from woe, free from desire, after having secluded themselves, those I call Munis.'[226]

[200]3. [1078] 'All these Samaṇas and Brāhmaṇas,' — so said the venerable Nanda, — 'say that purity comes from (philosophical) views, and from tradition, and from virtue and (holy) works, and in many (other) ways. Did they, in the way in which they lived in the world, cross over birth and old age, O venerable man? I ask thee, O Bhagavat, tell me this.'

4. [1079] 'All these Samaṇas and Brāhmaṇas, O Nanda,' — so said Bhagavat, — 'say that purity comes from (philosophical) views, and from tradition, and from virtue and (holy) works, and in many (other) ways; still they did not, in the way in which they lived in the world, cross over birth and old age, so I say.'

5. [1080] 'All these Samaṇas and Brāhmaṇas,' — so said the venerable Nanda, — 'say that purity comes from (philosophical) views, and from tradition, and from virtue and (holy) works, and in many (other) ways; if thou, O Muni, sayest that such have not crossed the stream, who then in the world of gods and men crossed over birth and old age, O venerable man? I ask thee, O Bhagavat, tell me this.'

6. [1081] 'I do not say that all Samaṇas and Brāhmaṇas, O Nanda,' — so said Bhagavat, — 'are shrouded by birth and old age; those who, after leaving in this world what has been seen or heard or thought, and all virtue and (holy) works, after leaving everything of various kinds, after penetrating desire, are free from passion, such indeed I call men that have crossed the stream.'[227]

[201] 7. [1082] Nanda: 'I delight in these words of the great Isi; well expounded (by thee), O Gotama, is freedom from upadhi (i.e. Nibbāna); those who, after leaving in this world what has been seen or heard or thought, and all virtue and (holy) works, after leaving everything of various kinds, after penetrating desire, are free from passion, such I call men that have crossed the stream.' (1082)

 

9. Hemakamāṇavapukkhā

[pali] [than]

 

1. [1083] 'Those who before in another world,' — so said the venerable Hemaka, — 'explained to me the doctrine of Gotama, saying, "So it was, so it will be," all that (was only) oral tradition, all that (was only) something that increased (my) doubts.[228]

2. [1084] 'I took no pleasure in that, but tell thou me the Dhamma that destroys desire, O Muni, which if a man has understood, and he wanders about thoughtful, he may cross desire in the world.'

3. [1085] Buddha: 'In this world (much) has been seen, [202] heard, and thought; the destruction of passion and of wish for the dear objects that have been perceived, O Hemaka, is the imperishable state of Nibbāna.

4. [1086] 'Those who, having understood this, are thoughtful, calm, because they have seen the Dhamma, tranquil and divine, such have crossed desire in this world.'[229]

 

10. Todeyyamāṇavapukkhā

[pali] [than]

 

1. [1087] 'He in whom there live no lusts,' — so said the venerable Todeyya, — 'to whom there is no desire, and who has overcome doubt, what sort of deliverance is there for him?'

2. [1088] 'He in whom there live no lusts, O Todeyya,' — so said Bhagavat, — 'to whom there is no desire, and who has overcome doubt, for him there is no other deliverance.'

3. [1089] Todeyya: 'Is he without breathing or is he breathing, is he possessed of understanding or is he forming himself an understanding?[230] Explain this to me, O thou all-seeing one, that I may know a Muni, O Sakka.'

[203] 4. [1090] Buddha: 'He is without breathing, he is not breathing, he is possessed of understanding, and he is not forming himself an understanding; know, O Todeyya, that such is the Muni, not possessing anything, not cleaving to lust and existence.'

 

11. Kappamāṇavapukkhā

[pali] [than]

 

1. [1091] 'For those who stand in the middle of the water,' — so said the venerable Kappa, — 'in the formidable stream that has set in, for those who are overcome by decay and death, tell me of an island, O venerable man, and tell thou me of an island that this (pain) may not again come on.'[231]

2. [1092] 'For those who stand in the middle of the water, O Kappa,' — so said Bhagavat, — 'in the formidable stream that has set in, for those overcome by decay and death, I will tell thee of an island, O Kappa.'

3. [1093] 'This matchless island, possessing nothing (and) grasping after nothing, I call Nibbāna, the destruction of decay and death.[232]

[204] 4. [1094] 'Those who, having understood this, are thoughtful (and) calm, because they have seen the Dhamma, do not fall into the power of Māra, and are not the companions of Māra.'

 

12. Gatukaṇṇimāṇavapukkhā

[pali] [than]

 

1. [1095] 'Having heard of a hero free from lust,' — so said the venerable Gatukannin, — 'who has crossed the stream, I have come to ask him who is free from lust; tell me the seat of peace, O thou with the born eye (of wisdom), tell me this truly, O Bhagavat.

2. [1096] 'For Bhagavat wanders about after having conquered lust as the hot sun (conquers) the earth by its heat; tell the Dhamma to me who has (only) little understanding, O thou of great understanding, that I may ascertain how to leave in this world birth and decay.'

3. [1097] 'Subdue thy greediness for sensual pleasures, O Gatukannin,' — so said Bhagavat, — 'having considered the forsaking of the world as happiness, let there not be anything either grasped after or rejected by thee.[233]

4. [1098] 'What is before thee, lay that aside; let there be nothing behind thee; if thou wilt not grasp after what is in the middle, thou wilt wander calm.[234]

[205] 5. [1099] 'For him whose greediness for name and form is wholly gone, O Brāhmaṇa, for him there are no passions by which he might fall into the power of death.'

 

13. Bhadrāvudhamāṇavapukkhā

[pali] [than]

 

1. [1100] 'I entreat the wise (Buddha), the houseless, who cuts off desire,' — so (said) the venerable Bhadravudha, — 'who is free from commotion, forsakes joy, has crossed the stream, is liberated, and who leaves time behind; having heard the chief's (word), they will go away from here.[235]

2. [1101] 'Different people have come together from the provinces, longing (to hear) thy speech, O hero; do thou expound it thoroughly to them, for this Dhamma is well known to thee.'

3. [1102] 'Let one wholly subdue the desire of grasping (after everything), O Bhadravudha,' — so said Bhagavat, — 'above, below, across, and in the middle; for whatever they grasp after in the world, just by that Māra follows the man.

4. [1103] 'Therefore, knowing this, let not the thoughtful Bhikkhu grasp after anything in all the world, considering as creatures of desire this generation, sticking fast in the realm of death.'

[206]

 

14. Udayamāṇavapukkhā

[pali] [than]

 

1. [1104] 'To Buddha who is sitting meditating, free from pollution,' — so said the venerable Udaya, — 'having performed his duty, who is without passion, accomplished in all things (dhamma), I have come with a question; tell me the deliverance by knowledge, the splitting up of ignorance.'

2. [1105] '(It consists in) leaving lust and desire, O Udaya,' — so said Bhagavat, — 'and both (kinds of) grief, and driving away sloth, and warding off misbehaviour.

3. [1106] 'The deliverance by knowledge which is purified by equanimity and thoughtfulness and preceded by reasoning on Dhamma I will tell thee, the splitting up of ignorance.'[236]

4. [1107] Udaya: 'What is the bond of the world, what is its practice? By the leaving of what is Nibbāna said to be?'[237]

5. [1108] Buddha: 'The world is bound by pleasure, reasoning is its practice; by the leaving of desire Nibbāna is said to be.'

6. [1109] Udaya: 'How does consciousness cease in him that wanders thoughtful? Having come to ask thee, let us hear thy words.'

[207] 7. [1110] Buddha: 'For him who both inwardly and outwardly does not delight in sensation, for him who thus wanders thoughtful, consciousness ceases.'

 

15. Posālamāṇavapukkhā

[pali] [than]

 

1. [1111] 'He who shows the past (births, etc),' — so said the venerable Posala, — 'who is without desire and has cut off doubt, to him who is accomplished in all things (dhamma), I have come supplicatingly with a question.

2. [1112] 'O Sakka, I ask about his knowledge who is aware of past shapes, who casts off every corporeal form, and who sees that there exists nothing either internally or externally; how can such a one be led (by anybody)?[238]

3. [1113] 'Tathāgata, knowing all the faces of consciousness, O Posala,' — so said Bhagavat, — 'knows (also) him who stands delivered, devoted to that (object).[239]

4. [1114] 'Having understood that the bonds of pleasure do not originate in nothingness (?), he sees clearly in [208] this (matter), this (is) the knowledge of a perfect, accomplished Brāhmaṇa.'[240]

 

16. Mogharāgamāṇavapukkhā

[pali] [than]

 

1. [1115] 'Twice have I asked Sakka,' — so said the venerable Mogharagan, — 'but the clearly-seeing has not explained it to me; if the divine Isi is asked for the third time, he will explain it, so I have heard.

2. [1116] 'There is this world, the other world, Brahman's world together with the world of the gods; I do not know thy view, the famous Gotama's (view).

3. [1117] 'To this man who sees what is good I have come supplicatingly with a question: How is any one to look upon the world that the king of death may not see him?'

4. [1118] 'Look upon the world as void, O Mogharagan, being always thoughtful; having destroyed the view of oneself (as really existing), so one may overcome death; the king of death will not see him who thus regards the world.'[241]

[209]

 

17. Piŋgiyamāṇavapukkhā

[pali] [than]

 

1. [1119] 'I am old, feeble, colourless,' — so said the venerable Piŋgiya, — 'my eyes are not clear, my hearing is not good; lest I should perish a fool on the way, tell me the Dhamma, that I may know how to leave birth and decay in this world.'

2. [1120] 'Seeing others afflicted by the body, O Piŋgiya,' — so said Bhagavat, — '(seeing) heedless people suffer in their bodies; — therefore, O Piŋgiya, shalt thou be heedful, and leave the body behind, that thou mayest never come to exist again.'

3. [1121] Piŋgiya: 'Four regions, four intermediate regions, above and below, these are the ten regions; there is nothing which has not been seen, heard, or thought by thee, and (is there) anything in the world not understood (by thee)? Tell (me) the Dhamma, that I may know how to leave birth and decay in this world'

4. [1122] 'Seeing men seized with desire, O Piŋgiya,' — so said Bhagavat, — 'tormented and overcome by decay, — therefore thou, O Piŋgiya, shalt be heedful, and leave desire behind, that thou mayest never come to exist again.'

 

 

This said Bhagavat, living in Magadha at Pāsāṇaka Ketiya (the Rock Temple). Sought by sixteen Brāhmaṇas, the followers (of Bāvarī, and) questioned by each of them in turn, he responded to the questions. If a man, having understood the meaning and tenor of each question, lives according to the Dhamma, then he will go to the further shore of decay and death, for these Dhammas lead to the [210] further shore, and therefore this order of Dhamma was called 'the way to the other shore.'

1., [1123] 2. [1124] Agita, Tissametteyya, Puṇṇaka and Mettagū, Dhotaka and Upasīva, Nanda and Hemaka, the two Todeyya and Kappa, and the wise Gatukaṇṇin, Bhadrāvudha and Udaya, and also the Brāhmaṇa Posāla, and the wise Mogharāgan, and Piŋgiya the great Isi.

3. [1125] These went up to Buddha, the Isi of exemplary conduct; asking subtle questions they went up to the supreme Buddha.

4. [1126] Buddha, being asked, responded to their questions truly, and in responding to the questions the Muni delighted the Brāhmaṇas.

5. [1127] They, having been delighted by the clearly-seeing Buddha, the kinsman of the Ādikkas, devoted themselves to a religious life near the man of excellent understanding.

6. [1128] He who lived according to what had been taught by Buddha (in answer) to each single question, went from this shore to the other shore.

7. [1129] From this shore he went to the other shore entering upon the most excellent way; this way is to lead to the other shore, therefore it is called 'the way to the other shore.'

8. [1130] 'I will proclaim accordingly the way to the further shore,' — so said the venerable Piŋgiya; — 'as he saw it, so he told it; the spotless, the very wise, the passionless, the desireless lord, for what reason should he speak falsely?

9. [1131] 'Well! I will praise the beautiful voice of (Buddha), who is without stain and folly, and who has left behind arrogance and hypocrisy.

10. [1132] 'The darkness-dispelling Buddha, the all-seeing, [211] who thoroughly understands the world,[242] has overcome all existences, is free from passion, has left behind all pain, is rightly called (Buddha), he, O Brāhmaṇa, has come to me.

11. [1133] 'As the bird, having left the bush, takes up his abode in the fruitful forest, even so I, having left men of narrow views, have reached the great sea, like the hamsa.[243]

12. [1134] 'Those who before in another world explained the doctrine of Gotama, saying, "So it was, so it will be," all that was only oral tradition, all that was only something that increased my doubts.[244]

13. [1135] 'There is only one abiding dispelling darkness, that is the high-born, the luminous, Gotama of great understanding, Gotama of great wisdom,

14. [1136] 'Who taught me the Dhamma, the instantaneous, the immediate, the destruction of desire, freedom from distress, whose likeness is nowhere.'[245]

15. [1137] Bāvarī: 'Canst thou stay away from him even for a moment, O Piŋgiya, from Gotama of great understanding, from Gotama of great wisdom, (1137).

[212] 16. [1138] 'Who taught thee the Dhamma, the instantaneous, the immediate, the destruction of desire, freedom from distress, whose likeness is nowhere?'

17. [1139] Piŋgiya: 'I do not stay away from him even for a moment, O Brāhmaṇa, from Gotama of great understanding, from Gotama of great wisdom,

18. [1140] 'Who taught me the Dhamma, the instantaneous, the immediate, the destruction of desire, freedom from distress, whose likeness is nowhere.

19. [1141] 'I see him in my mind and with my eye, vigilant, O Brāhmaṇa, night and day; worshipping I spend the night, therefore I think I do not stay away from him.

20. [1142] 'Belief and joy, mind and thought incline me towards the doctrine of Gotama; whichever way the very wise man goes, the very same I am inclined to.[246] (?)

21. [1143] 'Therefore, as I am worn out and feeble, my body does not go there, but in my thoughts I always go there, for my mind, O Brāhmaṇa, is joined to him.

22. [1144] 'Lying in the mud (of lusts) wriggling, I jumped from island to island; then I saw the perfectly Enlightened, who has crossed the stream, and is free from passion.'

23. [1145] Bhagavat:[247] 'As Vakkali was delivered by [213] faith, (as well as) Bhadrāvudha and Āḷavi-Gotama, so thou shalt let faith deliver thee, and thou shalt go, O Piŋgiya, to the further shore of the realm of death.'[248]

24. [1146] Piŋgiya: 'I am highly pleased at hearing the Muni's words; Sambuddha has removed the veil, he is free from harshness, and wise.

25. [1147] 'Having penetrated (all things) concerning the gods, he knows everything of every description; the Master will put an end to all questions of the doubtful that (will) admit (him).

26. [1148] 'To the insuperable, the unchangeable (Nibbāna), whose likeness is nowhere, I shall certainly go; in this (Nibbāna) there will be no doubt (left) for me, so know (me to be) of a dispossessed mind.'[249]

 

[1] Sir M. Coomāra Swāmy's translation of part of the book has been a great help to me. I hope shortly to publish the Pali text.

[2] C reads pagghasi.

[3] Cf. Indian Antiquary, 1880, p. 158.

[4] I am not sure whether sīlavata is to be understood as one notion or two. It is generally written in one word, but at p. 109 Vāseṭṭha says, when one is virtuous and endowed with works, he is a Brāhmaṇa, yato kho bho sīlavā ka hoti vatasampanno ka ettāvatā kho brāhmaṇo hoti. Sīlavata, I presume, refers chiefly to the Brāhmaṇas.

[5] From v. 456 we see that Buddha has rightly read vareṇiyam as the metre requires; but I must not omit to mention that the Commentator understands by Sāvittī the Buddhistic formula: Buddhaɱ saraṇaɱ gakkhāmi, Dhammaɱ saraṇaɱ gakkhāmi, Saŋghaɱ saraṇaɱ gakkhāmi, which, like Sāvitti, contains twenty-four syllables.

[6] Besides the religious Brāhmaṇas some secular Brāhmaṇas are mentioned, p. 11.

[7] Buddha is sometimes styled the great Isi, vv. 1060, 1082; sometimes a Muni, vv. 164, 700; sometimes a Brāhmaṇa, v. 1064; sometimes a Bhikkhu, vv. 411, 415; and all these appellations are used synonymously, vv. 283, 284, 1064, 1056, 843, 844, 911, 912, 946, 220. Ascetic life is praised throughout the book, especially in the Uraga-, Muni-, Rāhula-, Sammāparibbāganiya-, Dhammika-, Nālaka-, Purābheda-, Tuvaṭaka-, Attadaṇḍa-, and Sāriputta-suttas.

[8] This system ends, it will be seen from this, like other ascetic systems, in mysticism.

[246]

Saddhā ka pīti ka mano sati ka||
Nāmenti me Gotamasāsanamhā (?),||
Yaɱ yaɱ disaɱ vagati bhūripañño||
Sa tena ten'eva nato 'ham asmi.|| ||

[247] At the conclusion of this (i.e. the preceding) gatha, Bhagavat, who stayed at Sāvatthī, when seeing the maturity of the minds of Piŋgiya and Bāvarī, shed a golden light. Piŋgiya, who sat picturing Buddha's virtues to Bāvarī, having seen the light, looked round, saying, 'What is this?' And when he saw Bhagavat standing, as it were, before him, he said to the Brāhmaṇa Bāvarī: 'Buddha has come.' The Brāhmaṇa rose from his seat and stood with folded hands. Bhagavat, shedding a light, showed himself to the Brāhmaṇa, and knowing what was beneficial for both, he said this stanza while addressing Piŋgiya. Commentator.
